And it is called "aspre cap", and it is made of iron, and its head is rounded like an ash tree which is pierced by a punch or calipers for a subtle point (such as it is in the following form).
And place that on the corrupted part of the bone, and then revolve it between those same things, and press your hand little by little until you know that corruption is scraped away.
And then be some times, and then restore the place with causes of desiccation and astringency.
Therefore if the place is consolidated and good flesh is born in it, and the body of pus is removed and remains for 40 days, and there is no renewal and no apostema, and no accident occurs in it, then you will know that it is healed.
And otherwise, then it is not in it that engendering occurs, but rather reducing the fissure to the form of the nose, and according to this manner: and it is that you uncover the second bone with iron or acute medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a thread according to this form:
Be the extremity of the iron triangular, and the stem of it first be attenuated up to the extremity in such a way that it is like that many times.
And then place that on the bone, and let it be just above the lacrimal; and stretch your hand a little from the eye, and then revolve your hand with the thread until it penetrates all the bone, and that feels the patient by as much as it finds a way to go out because of when you put your hand.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent and desiccating medicines, just as if they had been ordered.
Therefore when the flesh and the place are hardened, then the material which flowed to the outside parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier on the patient.
Twentieth Chapter.
On the Reduction of the Eminence of the Eye.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety and does not cause harm or diminution to the surrounding parts, then it is necessary that you lax the patient, and then follow this, and then pause breathing exercises in the neck without scarification and suction for good suction; and then pause on the eye a liniment made of balsam and aloe and onions and sarcocolla, and then strengthen the poultice on the eye with many powders.
And the manner of the powders is that you take strips of lion's claw, and each strip be doubled into four folds; and make from those materials.
And then place those one on top of the other according to the quantity that the eye requires. And then strengthen on those with a strong cloth and firm constriction; and iterate the cupping glasses with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye tightly bound day and night.
But if the ligament is softened meanwhile, then it is necessary that it be made quite tight.
And then remove the ligament day and night just as we have said.
And if the eye is already reduced, good; and otherwise, then iterate the poultice with powders and constriction and cupping glasses until it is healed.
Twenty-First Chapter.
On the Incision of the Eminence of the Uveal Tunic.
When there is disrupcion in the uveal tunic, and it becomes prominent and proceeds outside the eyelids just like a grain of raisin, and the shape of a man is formed by this, and you want to incise it, then the operation in it is as we have recounted, that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from the side below, and then place another needle on the double thread of the part of the lacrimal, and make that penetrate and leave the first needle according to its disposition; and then incise the location of the double thread and lift up with that the part of the uvea in the circle of the needle, and strengthen it firmly.
And then extract the needle and place on the eye lanolin infused in egg white, and let it stay until the thread falls away from the uvea.
And then cure the eye with that which comforts it, until the wound is healed.
And some times gather from all parts with a circle of cloth into the eye, and then be perforated by a subtle spatula that comes to the very front of the eye, and flow the albugineal humidity, and let the eye descend and enter immediately.
And then strengthen it until it is healed.
Twenty-Second Chapter.
On the Cure of Alconatis.
This illness which is called alconati is nothing but pus that is adjusted in the eye similar to descending water, and it is not that.
But the manner of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight bed, and then take your own head in two hands and press that into the part where you see the pus coming out, and then be dried and not descend, and be stopped from shining, and the patient sees the causes just as he sees.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the pus is not prepared with that which we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and take a subtle spatula and find it on the panniculus clefting a little way from the continuation of the conjunctiva to the corneal tunica in the crown (into the location you see), until the pus is forgotten.
Therefore when it is forgotten, then you will distill in the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey and water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then be cured by other causes until it is healed.
Twenty-Second Chapter.
On the Cure of the Water Descending from the Eye.
We have already said the species of water in the division and which is suitable to pierce by saying it clearly, then that according to health.
Then you must make the patient sit between your hands on a couch and bind their own eye tightly with a string.
And then place your right hand just above the patient's left eyelid if the eye with the water is the left one; or with your right hand if the eye is the right one.
And then place your left hand if the eye is left; or with your right hand if the eye is right.
And then place the extremity of the almond just above the crown by the thickness of the rim in the albomen of the eye from the smaller lacrimal part, and then squeeze the almond with force and revolve your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the albomen of the eye and feel that the almond has already reached its intended location, visible to the eye.
And it is necessary that the measure of the path of the almond to the front be equal to the measure of the space which is that of the pupil from the beginning of darkness, and it is the crown of the eye; for you will see with it the pupil through the vision of the eye by the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the location where the water is, and then you will draw it down one time another, and thus if the water descends soon, then the patient will see that condition over which their sight will be affected soon, and the almond in its own eye.
And then pause for a little while; thus if the water has descended, then you will deposit that other one before the almond is dropped.
And thus when it is dried and does not descend, then you will extract the almond with ease, and revolve your hand with it little by little, and then dissolve a small amount of clear gemstone salt in that water and wash the eye outward; and then pause outside the eye coto or stop or lanolin infused in rose oil and egg white, and bind the eye with it.
But as for us, those of our time place on that which is already mixed with egg white.
And if it does not obey your almond at the entrance of the eye due to its hardness - for it is one of those men whose eyes are very hard - then you must take the spatula which is called alberid, and this is its form:
And then pierce with it that conjuncture, and do not stop your hand in the hole, and it is not so unless you make a way for the almond to go into its subtle location.
And then introduce the almond according to what we have said.
And thus complete the complement of your work, prepare the patient for sleep, making them descend into a dark house, and prohibit all movements.
And pause in the nature of that water so that its nature is aligned, and do not bend their head to either the right or left side at all.
And let the ligature be according to its disposition within three days.
And then remove that in the darkest way and show forth the most fervent vision, and let it see some causes, and then return to the ligature on the 7th day.
And do not make this in the hour of cures or soon after an infusion. They had better that you leave it like that, because the water will flow more easily for great and vehement regard.
But if it happens that a pustule forms, then you must remove the eye seven days before, and correct that with water which settles on the pustule.
Then you must absolve the vision of him and pause on his face orally by which it is governed from behind for several days, existing in the darkest way.
And then be guided gradually out of that darkness and converse with his own causes.
And know that in each other's company there is no excuse for the disciple to see some things occasionally, and then you will be bold over the operation.
And it is already reached us of some of those who are at Artach, and some said that fire was made in the Ayrac magnificently perforated by which the water flows.
But I do not see anything like it in our land which has perfected this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this may be new.
And these are the forms of the species of magic by which it is on those who do not know about them:
And they are made of iron or bronze properly, and they are the extremities of their own with this subtlety triangular, the sharp tip just like that is the form.
Twenty-Fourth Chapter.
On the Cure of Nostrils in Cartilage.
When in the nostrils are born various carnages adjusted from which is some cause which is assembled to Scorpio of heavy weight or to Polyp feet; and this illness is called Polypous because it resembles that feet represented. And from it is born a cancerous flesh, pale and of dark color; and from it is born a lion-like flesh not of dark color.
Then in that which is in these lion-like flesh not fraudulent or cancerous, it is necessary to make the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, and anoint their nostrils with oil, and pick out once in those fleshy parts. And then extract that from the outside parts, and then incise it with what you have comprehended from those with a sharp, subtle spatula from one part until you know that all the flesh is removed; for if there remains some cause from which abscess is possible, then you must ratify this with one of the subtle instruments with ease so that no cause remains. But if bleeding occurs or an abscess forms, then you must pause in what is necessary; or it is one of the malignant apostemes, then you must cut and cauterize that until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are removed.
And then be guided in the nose just like the incision, with water or vinegar. But if the nose is overflowing and moisture proceeds into the palate, then you will know that it has already been healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you will know that behind it is flesh nothing in the uppermost bones of the nose to which abscess has not been brought.
Therefore it is necessary to introduce faithfully the thread which has some thickness, and push into each nostril the quantity of the said or less, and generate the illness and introduce one extremity of the thread into the nose with the ray or with another cause which is possible to it.
And once this has been done, then take your own perfumed substance and draw it into the canals of the nose, and let it be thus on the palate, because many times children at school seem to have a similar action, and it is a light cause for one who wants to do so.
And then pause the two extremities of the thread, one extremity that comes out above the nose and the other that remains in the nose, and then administer a binding of flesh with the threads that are in the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is bound to the threads of the thread.
And then extract the thread, and pause in the nose about one quarter of an inch away from the bleeding, and do this for three days or more until the ulcerated part is completely removed from the flesh.
And then at the end of the cause, pause in the nose with a leaden cannula for several days until it is healed.
But if you are out of cure that exists, use this one.
And this is the form of the instrument, the capipurge maker, with which oils and medicines are distilled in the nose:
And make it of silver or bronze, which is like a small lamp open, just like a small shell, and the mouth similarly.
And if you want, make a closed cannula like a cane, and the cannula of the instrument to make capipurge and be flat, uncovered; to which there is a handle at the end of it, with which you will hold that when you heat it in oil or water that you want from the juices and causes susceptible.
Twenty-Fifth Chapter.
On the Nature of Moles in the Extremities of the Nose.
Moles are often found in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in some days until the mole is seen to be a manifestation of its regard.
And therefore it is necessary that you incise that in the beginning of the appearance of it, and eradicate all that. And then pause on the spot or cauterize or apply medicine which is present in that place.
But if it is delayed in its incision, then consider whether it is lumpy, hard, of dark color and little feeling, then do not touch that with iron because it is a cancerous abscess; for many times he who incises this abscess devotes great malice against him whom it had.
And if the abscess is of a lion-like touch and not of dark color, and it is possible to incise in all that, then evacuate the illness and incise that without fear, and cure the location with what exists and constrain until it is healed.
Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
On the Suture of the Nose and Lips and Ear when Continuity is Dissolved by Wound or Similitude.
Know that when solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, few confer on that operation except in some men.
Therefore, when you intend to operate on some cause of those, consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then you should adjust the two lips of the wound with suture, and appropriately cure that until it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is made whole, then it is necessary that you excoriate each part of its apparent cord until there is no blood. And then adjust the two lips with suture and strengthen those, and upon those powder blood of dragon and three times thrice, and pause on the powder one poultice diaphanicon or some other consolidating poultice, and bind that there for two days or three, and then remove that and replace that poultice and bind that until the threads are cut off, and then cure with a gentle touch until it is healed.
But the manner of suture is that you add the solution of continuity or with needles just as we have recounted in the suture of the belly, or add the solution of continuity with thread just as to your own and make it known.
Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
On the Extraction of those causes which come to the two lips in the parts behind.
Many times it happens to some men in the parts behind their lips that certain abscesses hard and resembling flesh appear, and some of theirs are smaller and some are greater.
Therefore it is necessary that you convert the lips and openings of each of those hardenings throughout its part, and then fill the abscess with sage thrice until the blood is restrained, and then wash with vinegar and cure the location with what exists and is stipticitat, until it is healed.
Twenty-Eighth Chapter.
On the Incision of the Adjusted Flesh in the Gums.
Great many times the flesh of the gum is adjusted which the ancients called albulus, therefore it is necessary that you suspend that with one ounce or that you hold that with the forceps and incise that as much as its reason demands, and let the blood flow freely.
And then pause on the spot with sage thrice or other powders stiptics or excitants.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it secondarily, and cauterize it, for then the cautery will not return.
Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Reason of Teeth with Iron.
When in the surface of the teeth of the posterior parts and those of the front and between the gums are adjusted, rough, sharp, and some times blackened and made citrines or greenish so that the gingiva is corrupted and the teeth become loose.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and pause the head in your own seat, and ratify the teeth in which those roughnesses and causes resembling sand appear, so that none of their causes remain, and this will be done with the blackness and citrinity and other things until they are purified.
Therefore if this is what is at the beginning of the reason, good; and otherwise, then you must iterate the reason over those on another day and on the second and on the third until it comes to the final statement in what you want.
And know that teeth have required many remedies of diverse forms and figures according to the preparation for your operation. And therefore because the remedies with which the tooth is razed from the posterior part are another of those with which it is rasped between teeth according to other forms, and these forms of remedies must be prepared by you which you will see soon:
Thirtieth Chapter.
On the Eradication of Teeth.
It is necessary that you cure the tooth of its pain with all care and diligence in total eradication of it, for there is no cause which supplies the growth of it when it is eradicated, for it is subtle substance within which is excuse in the eradication of it.
But it is necessary that when the illness attacks that tooth to be eradicated, you must firmly certify which is the aching tooth, for many times pain descends from the illness and takes hold in a healthy tooth, and you eradicate that one and then pause until the diseased and aching tooth is eradicated, for we have already seen this through the operation of barberries.
Therefore since you must certify which is the aching tooth and know well, it is necessary that you scarify in the circuit of the tooth with spatium in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth of the gingiva on all sides, and then proceed to remove that with your own words or with subtle forceps little by little until those things are consumed.
And then proceed with great force to hold fast the large grasping power for good conversation or strong persuasion, and pause the head of the illness between your own denials that you hold well so as not to trouble it, and then gently remove the tooth according to rectitude in manner so as not to break it or give greater pain.
And therefore if it is not forgotten, then take one of those instruments and place it beside the tooth on all sides with facility and effort, so that it is done as at the beginning.
But if the tooth is fractured or corroded, then it is necessary that you wrap with cloth the fracture and strengthen it with a thin thread at the end of a fine ray, so that it will not be broken in the hour of your constriction upon it as you hold it.
And it is necessary that you work extremely carefully with scarification in the circuit of all parts of the gingiva and guard your strength so as not to break it and leave a part of it, and return upon the illness through the malice which is greater than the original pain.
And beware that you do not do as some ignorant barbers in their folly and audacity over the eradication of their own, without administering what we have recounted; for many times they bring upon men great illnesses which are more difficult to be broken, and the roots of their decayed teeth are all removed, or some of those, or that you extract it with certain bones of the jawbone just as many times we have testified.
And then the eradication of it is washed away with vinegar or with hot water and salt.
And if there is bleeding afterwards, just as many times it happens, then take some quantity of sage and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that sage does not agree with you, then cauterize.
The form of the forceps and fine tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be of long extra thumb, short handle and thick, so that they are not bent when you must work with them.
And should be like times of thick handle so that when you strain with those they are not bent, and the extra thumbs should be short.
And should be made of Indian iron with descending handles shaped like right-handed thumbs, and on the handles of those there should be teeth which some enter into others and strain through tight construction, and sometimes the handles of those are in the shape of a bird's beak called ciconia, so it is guarded, and they have strong constriction.
Thirty-First Chapter.
On the Eradication of Teeth and Extraction of Bones from Gnawed Jaws.
When you must eradicate the tooth which is gnawed by some cause, it is necessary that you pause over the spot with a border for one day or two until the spot is mollified; and then proceed to grasp with gentle fingers or tongs the extremities of those which are assembled like the beak of a bird called ciconia or guanta, the shape of the forceps:
The extremities of them should be made downwards just like lime or alfage.
And if they do not obey your eye with these forceps or tongs, it is necessary that you cut above the root and uncover all the flesh and enter the instrument which has no name, it being a small lever whose form is this:
And should be of short extremity, thick and small, and not be surrounded in order that it will not break.
Therefore if through this the root is forgotten, good; and otherwise, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is triangular extremity in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and with a more subtle extremity.
And already you are helping yourself with this instrument having two rams of which this is the form, approximately like the following: and with other instruments which precede in tooth scraping; for already it is made with this instrument which is assembled like once great, and this is the form:
of the triangular extremity twisted or short, which has some thickness, and not be surrounded in order that it will not break.
And another thing you should know is that the instruments for teeth are made of one piece and similarly to other instruments.
But the skilled and subtle craftsmen in the art, find for themselves according to what to them means the operation and various illnesses; for there are illnesses to which the ancients did not know nor pause proper instruments, by the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of other bones in the mouth is broken or split, then it is necessary that you carefully place a weight upon it in its location and in a way that is convenient for the instruments and forceps which we have said in the extraction of the roots of teeth, and help yourself with gestures of which this is the form:
And should be in it some thickness little by little, and should be wrapped or held in the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone of all is forgotten; and then proceed to restore the location with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small crack in the bone, then it is necessary to remove that crack and its blackness, and cure that until it is healed.
Thirty-Second Chapter.
On the Cure of Teeth Elevated above Others.
When the teeth are elevated above others, not according to their natural form, and more so when it concerns women, and most of all venerable old ladies; therefore it is necessary that you consider: if a tooth is too close to another tooth, and there is no possibility of suture or union, then remove that one; but if it is annexed to another tooth, then you should incise that one with this instrument whose form is as follows; and it resembles the snout of a pig, and is made of Indian iron with an extremely sharp extremity.
And be your incision at which you should incise that one in many days due to the hardness of the tooth, and so that it will not become like another tooth.
And if an elevated tooth is exposed to decay, then remove that one with a file of Indian iron whose form is as follows:
And be all of Indian iron and the edge slightly curved; and be made of many small points like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed gradually in many days with ease, so that it will not become another tooth that is decayed and spoils. And then file that one at the end, and remove that one with some rasps.
And if a tooth is broken and the tongue lies against it, then it is necessary that you file that one until the sharpness of that fracture is removed and made smooth, and be filled in order that the tongue does not lie against it nor corrupt the word.
Thirty-Third Chapter.
On the Restoration of Decayed Teeth with Gold or Silver File.
When accidents happen to teeth before concussion and removal by percussion or falling, and the illness cannot strengthen over what is decayed, and you have cured that one with purgative medicines, and your cure has not been effective, then in order that they do not decay further, it is necessary to strengthen those with a gold file and not silver, for silver turns green and is soon spoiled, but gold never changes.
And be the file in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the form of the application is that you take the file and take twice as much of it between two healthy teeth, and then increase with two twists of the file between two decayed ones, or if one or many, until the thread reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then iterate the file into the part where you began, and strengthen your work with ease and wisdom until it is not decayed.
And be your constriction at which you should strengthen the file around the roots of the teeth so that they do not escape.
And then incise the superfluous extremities of the file with forceps or pincers, and add those in each place with gestures, and convert them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the decayed one, so that they do not cause injury to the tongue. And then loosen that one as straight as possible.
But if it is loosened or split, strengthen that one or those with another file in such a way for all its time.
And this is the form of teeth and figure of the correction of two healthy teeth and two decayed ones, just as these are linked:
And when one returns or two teeth to their places, and are regrown just as we have recounted, and remain.
And only good craftsmen and wise ones do this.
And sometimes it is suitable for some cause of cow bones, and it is made from them as a form of tooth, and it is placed in a location where the tooth falls out, and it is strengthened just as we have said, and remains and serves that one for a long time.
Thirty-Fourth Chapter.
On the Incision of the Ligament which appears in the Tongue and prohibits Speech.
This ligament which appears on the tongue some times or is natural to it, or accidental and occurs by wound which has already been consolidated.
And the operation in this case is that you open the mouth of the patient, and lift his tongue which exists in its place; then proceed to incise that ligament nervously according to the latitude so that the tongue may be freed from its recent recency.
But if in that case there is some hardness or concretion, and this has been established by the consolidation of the wound, then you must gently apply an unguent and dissect it according to the latitude until you have dissected the ligament and dissolved the concretion.
And beware that there is not a fissure made in the forehead of the flesh and that you dissect here artery and blood flow comes out.
And then wash the vestige of the incision with aqua regia or with hot water or with a gentle stream; and then pause leaving the tongue to relax; thus is the way that the patient leaves his tongue at night so that it will not be consolidated again.
But if there is a flow of blood, pause over the location with a red cloth pressed; but if the blood runs, then cauterize the location with a cauterizing instrument which corresponds to this. And then cure that one with other causes until it is healed.
Thirty-Fifth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Ranula below the Tongue.
Sometimes an abscess forms below the tongue, resembling a small tumor, which prohibits the tongue from its natural operation; and very soon it is magnified until it fills the mouth.
And the operation in this case is that you open the mouth of the patient, sitting in front of the sun and consider the abscess.
And if you see it is a dark red or black, hard abscess, which the patient does not feel any sensation from, then do not touch it because it is cancerous.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, with humidity, then you must put the onion in there, and incise that one with a subtle spatula, and incise it entirely; and if blood comes out during your operation, then pause over it with a red cloth pressed, so that the blood may be restrained.
And then return to your work in order to complete it there, until you have washed it with hot water and salt; and cure that one with the other cure, according to this, until it is healed.
Thirty-Sixth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Abscesses from Both Ears and that which is nothing in the Gullet and other Abscesses.
When it is swollen in the gullet, so are the glands which are assembled there, which burst forth and are called two ears, which when you cure them with what we have said in the division and they do not heal, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of dark red color, with little sensation, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red color and the root is large, then do not touch that one with iron for the flow of blood; but rather let that one be left until it has matured, and then either you will take it out or it will break apart by itself.
And if it is of white color, rough, and the root of it is subtle, then that one must be incised.
And the operation in this case is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already relaxed and has a complete repose or if it has diminished by some diminution; and then make the patient sit down in front of the sun, with his head in its natural position, and open his mouth, and the servant takes hold between his own hands the tongue of the patient below with an instrument most noble whose form is as follows:
And be made of silver or iron, and be subtle like a knife.
Then when you take hold of that tongue and it is shown to you the abscess, and gaze upon that with your own sight, then take an onion and place it in the amenla, and pull that out as far as you can. And then other thing which pulls some cause of the tunics, and then incise that one with an instrument whose form is as follows:
And then other thing which pulls some cause of the tunics, and then incise that one with an instrument whose form is as follows: and is assembled like forceps, however the extremities of it are curved, so that the orifice of each of those is in the end of the other, very sharp, made of Indian iron thoroughly surrounded.
But if this instrument is not with you at present, then incise that one with a spatula whose form is as follows: And be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one notch, incise the other in the same manner of incision, and then the incision will cause the patient to spit out water.
But if there is bleeding, cause the patient to spit out water of decoction of sorrel or mint leaves and similar things to those causes which are stiptic, until the flow of blood is restrained, and then cure that one until it is healed.
And sometimes there are other abscesses in the gullet besides those two ears, and incise them according to what I have said in the incision of two notches equally.
And I had already treated a woman with an abscess which was located in her gullet, which abscess tended towards dark red color, with little sensation, which had already closed up the gullet of hers, and she was breathing with great difficulty, and had already prohibited food and drink from water, and was on the verge of death if I had not acted within a day or two.
And the abscess had already grown so large that it had eaten away two fingers' width, and they were about to burst through two holes.
Then I cut the skin forcibly and stayed in one of them with an onion, and pulled that out, and drew it is from a great portion, and then I incised that one where I understood the other hole, and then I obliged the patient's mouth, and compressed his tongue, and then I placed the onion in that abscess.
And then I abstracted a great part of it, and there was only a small amount of blood flowing from it. Therefore the gullet of that woman was cleared, and she began to drink water, and then I prescribed food, so that I did not cease to incise that abscess many times for a long time, and succeeded in incising another abscess in its place, which extended until it was prolonged.
Therefore I ended up here and cauterized the gullet of her, so that after addition; and then I wandered and did not know what God had done to me through that.
Thirty-Seventh Chapter.
On the Incision of the Uvea when it is Abscessed.
When the catarrh settles on the uvea, and it is abscessed and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the posterior part, large and rounded, then it is called a boil.
But when it is cured with the water that we have said in the division, and you do not confirm your cure, and it seems only once that the abscess has already been left behind and is subtle, then you must incise that one which is added rounded; and if it is to that length, and is bloody, of dark red color or black, and there is no sensation to it, then you must take great care in making the incision, for in that case there is fear over the patient.
And when you incise that one according to the manner which I have said of its whiteness and length, so that you make the patient sneeze and press his tongue with an instrument whose form is as follows: And be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And be careful not to incise too much, but only as far as is adjusted to the natural term without addition, for if you incise too much, it will impair the patient's speech.
And then use the same instrument which we have recounted in the incision of two notches, and cure that one until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, it is necessary to use force in the cauterization without fear, and the manner of the cauterizer in this case is nothing other than with sharp water, and it is that you pause the head of the patient in its natural position, and then press his tongue with the instrument which we have said, and take from the water which we have said in the chapter on cauterization, and confirm it with living cause, and pause that one not thick nor subtle, and fill the concavity of the instrument whose form is as follows:
So that the extremity of the concavity is in the shape of a spoon, like the concavity of a ladle, and pause the instrument with the medicine over the uvula, and the patient lies down on his back for so that the saliva or mucus of the mouth flows into the concavity through the intrinsic medicine, so that it does not descend from any cause onto the gullet of him and thus descends.
And then take your hand and the medicine, and express it over the uvula for the space of about half an hour until you see that one being blackened and sedated by the biting of the medicine.
And if you wish, take a cloth and wrap it around the extremity of the ladle, and submerge the cloth in the medicine, and insert the ladle with the cloth into a canula above, until the cloth adheres to that canula, and do this many times until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it alone for about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and then cauterize the abscess in the circuit of the uvula with a cloth wrapped around it, and clean out the excess medicine from the circuit of the abscess, and wash it with hot water, and apply an ointment externally, and internally with gargling until it is healed.
And this kind of cure is even safer than incision and longer in terms of fear.
And sometimes the uvula is cured with this method which is even more subtle than cauterization and incision, for it is treated through subfumigation in this manner: take calament, myrrh, origanum, ruda, Arabic scorched plants, camomile, and similar to those of herbs, and adjust all or some of these causes into a bath, and let them be submerged in hot water, and boil; and the bath should be well stirred with a decent stirring, and there should be a hole in the middle of the bath, above which is placed an instrument shaped like a spoon made of iron or steel.
And then insert the extremity into the mouth of the patient until the vapor reaches the uvula on the canal, until the uvula is very much obscured.
And then repeat this process many times until it is dry, and be careful not to do this treatment at the beginning of the appearance of abscesses, for too much pressure would be applied to the abscess, and you should not do that, but rather declare the presence of the abscess from the start.
But if you do not have this instrument, then take a reed and shape it at the extremity of the tube into a close of wood, so that the patient's mouth is closed off, for the closing of the wood prohibits the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Thirty-Eighth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Thorns and that which adheres to the gullet and other causes.
Many times there are thorns in the gullet or thorn-like growths from certain other causes.
Therefore it is necessary that you extract what appears to be from those things, upon which the sight falls, since you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it may be declared to you what is in the gullet.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the gullet, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before food is digested in the stomach, because very strongly the cause annexed to the gullet will be forgotten through vomiting; or that the patient drinks a glass of vinegar or of raw lettuce juice or a cup of stale bread; or takes a handful of dried seaweed sponge and binds it tightly with a thread, and then makes the patient swallow it, so that when it reaches the place where the thorns are and is extracted, then they will be forgotten. And also take an eel and bind it with a thread, and put it into the patient's mouth and then extract it; or take a pipe and insert it and then pull it out.
But if the causes we have said are not forgotten, then take an instrument of lead in this form:
And be a little thicker than a reed, and at the extremity of it have a concavity which you insert into the patient's gullet with ease.
And the instrument will lift up its head in that way, and be careful not to touch the patient's bone of the throat, for fear of causing trouble or injury to the patient's body, or that it might get stuck in his mouth.
But meanwhile, the patient will move it back and forth with his hand on the instrument, guided by his knowledge of the location of the annexed cause and the way it protrudes downwards, pulling his hand in sync with the movement of the instrument, all according to the amount that is prepared until it is forgotten.
Thirty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Blood-Clots attached to the Gullet.
When blood-clots are medicated with the water we have said in the division of the cure by medicines and it does not take effect, consider then that there is something attached to the gullet of the patient like the sun, for you should have compressed the tongue of him with the instrument which we have recounted.
Therefore, if you see the blood-clot, extract it gently or with subtle gestures without causing any disturbance.
And if it is not possible to extract it, then take a concave tube and insert it into the patient's gullet, and then pause in the concavity of the tube an iron point that has been heated, and do this many times (until the blood-clot is dead and falls out of the gullet) and abstain from giving the patient water all day.
And then take a full vial of hot water, and pour it into the patient's mouth, and wash his mouth with it; and do not allow any cause to be transmitted through it, but rather let the water sit for an hour with its own power, because the blood-clot will soon dissolve when it feels the water.
But if it is not forgotten with what we have recounted, then subfumigate the patient with cinnamom or with asafoetida through the instrument which we have recounted in the subfumigation of the uvula.
And do this, so that it will fall away.
And there is another method of operation in the subfumigation of this, which is that you take a bath in which are hot coals and cover it with a covering in the middle of which is a hole, and shape the extremity of the instrument in that hole; and then proceed to subfumigate; and pause the patient's mouth at the extremity of the tube, and close his mouth so that the subfumigation is not forgotten until you know that the subfumigation has reached the blood-clot, for the blood-clot will soon fall away.
And if it doesn't work, then repeat the subfumigation many times, and abstain from giving the patient water, and make him eat salty and hot causes, and not beg for water, and thus it is necessary that this regimen be forgotten.
And this is the form of the instrument with which blood-clots are extracted from the gullet when it is visible; and it is similar to forceps like these, but with some curvature. The one that is placed in the gullet, and its extremity is similar to a stork's beak, which is grasping, with a sharp edge of lime, so that when you compress any cause, it will not let go.
Fortieth Chapter.
In which is the summary of the sermon in warning about abscesses and their section.
The abscesses are many species, of many methods, according to the remembrance of each one in this book.
And thus diverse in perforation and section of them by two methods, of which one is by the species of abscesses themselves; and because they absorb moisture.
And the second method is proper for the locations where accidents occur in the body, because the abscess that occurs in the head is different from the abscess that occurs in the buttocks; and the abscess in the fleshy location is different from the abscess at the joint; and each of them has its own judgment by operation.
And there are some abscesses that do not need to be punctured, but rather should be allowed to mature on their own in those cases or until they complete their course.
So other abscesses that need to be punctured when they are crossed, and are not mature according to their completion, like those that are proper for joints, for when an abscess appears near a joint, the matter within it is prolonged until it becomes a pus-filled abscess in the circuit of the joint, and then the surrounding tissues or nerves become corrupted, causing contraction of that limb; or if it is an abscess next to the main member, and you delay puncturing it until it has matured too much for the main member; or it is an abscess near the anus, therefore puncture it unripe; and do not make a fresh incision, but only one that is proper for the anus, so that the pus does not spread to other parts of the body and becomes elongated outside the anus, and becomes a fistula, or is formed in the end of the abscess that is not healed. But it is necessary for you to know the hour of puncturing the abscess, and thus it is when it is mature.
And know that it is mature when there is the cessation of pain and fever, and also the decrease in swelling and pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And when it is necessary to know that the puncturing should occur in the location most distant from the abscess and closest to the cure of the prominence.
And the puncturing should be done through the length of the flesh or the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the muscles or of the thighs, and in places such as the lacertus, cords of nerves, arteries; and, finally, in all similar locations where there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use puncturing according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy location, then it is better that you wait for the puncturing to be confirmed through digestion according to completion, as we have said; for if you puncture too early, it will prolong the body's suffering and much annoyance, and thus the lips of the wound will become inflamed and its depth.
But some abscesses should be punctured according to the latitude of the flesh or body, depending on necessity, or according to that which the member requires.
And it is necessary to administer small punctures, and in large abscesses, a great puncture and many sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And there are others that are abscesses in which it is necessary to have the cord compressed and bound, when it is made so that it becomes a poultice, as with those that occur in troops of boils and buboes.
And there are others that have a cord bound with three sections; and of these, one is incised like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as with the abscesses of the gnomes.
And there are others that have sections that are round and lunar-shaped, resembling those.
But as for the one who does not have such abscesses like those of the lion, it is necessary that you puncture it with a superficial or flat puncture only.
And it is necessary when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, that you puncture it and do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted one part, then fill the abscess with another day, and then extract a larger part, and thus make many openings according to gradation until it is forgotten.
And most especially if the patient is of fresh virtue, or a woman in childbirth, or a young child, or an old person in decrepitude, for the animal spirit many times is resolved with the excretion of pus, and thus the patient soon dies, and you open them.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and proceed with your puncturing in the way that you puncture these abscesses, it is necessary to cleanse the wounds and consider then whether the wound of the abscess is small or a flat section; then you administer some lint or old cloth; but if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you must pause in each hole until they all penetrate together.
And if in the abscess a part of the cord is cut off or has been concave-shaped in the circuit, then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth or a piece of fabric made from linen threads, just as when you gather up bread or raisins, without moisture; and strengthen that for three days; and then proceed to treat it and cure it with water according to what is needed for the wound until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during the hour of your operation, then you must administer hot water and vinegar in which a cloth of linen has been soaked, and pause that over the location of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer remedies and treatments, and apply the powders which I have recommended in many places of this our book and division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then it is necessary that you envelop powders in vinegar and oil, and pause them over those locations.
And if it is in the summer, and the locations are fleshy, then it is necessary that you pause powders mixed with water and oil or vinegar and oil, and all this be hot until the third day, just as we have said, and then it is necessary to open the abscess and cleanse it and administer in its cure what is necessary for it until it is healed.
And thus is what is necessary for the knowledge of the universal cure of abscesses, but according to the way of distinction I have said for every kind of abscess in which manner is the path of its cure, chapter by chapter.
Fortieth Chapter.
On the Section of Abscesses which occur in the Cord of the Head.
In the cord of the head, small abscesses occur, and of species of nodes which contain tunics that are their own facts; and the species of these are many.
And among them are pustules, and of those that contain moisture similar to lime. And among them are those that contain moisture similar to powder, and resembling their own texture; and of those that are lithoid, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, as you must not make the extraction cause irritation of the arteries.
And the operation in section of those is that you express them with an instrument whose shape is similar to this, which is called an entromer, until you know what they contain.
Therefore, if what it contains is moisture, then you should cut it along with a section according to its length, by a superficial section in this form:
And begin at the section of the line .B. until the line .G. Then, as the moisture is evacuated, you should excoriate that part which contains that moisture, and do not leave any cause remaining, for many times it returns if some cause remains.
And then submerge the cloth in Egyptian ointment, if it is present, and if not, in water and salt; and fill it with that which fills the wound and leave it for another day, for at that time you should corrode all that remains of that peel.
And then iterate on that cloth with Egyptian ointment secondarily and thirdly if you need it, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that peel; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you should incise that according to this form: with a section having the form of a cross, and cut once between two lips of the section and scrape that out of all part, and exert your virtue in extracting the tunic which contains that.
But if to your arteries it makes contrariety, then do what I have previously recounted first.
And similarly do in the lithoid abscess of section and cure according to what we have said; and section on lithoid abscess is less troublesome, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And in some cases, there are certain of these abscesses which are in the head of some old women who have little or no moisture; the cause of this is that I once incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and extracted it as if it were a deaf, hard, white, and rough stone, which could not be moved, and if anyone had struck it with a hammer, it would have hurt.
But in other abscesses that occur on the head, this is not the same as this chapter, such as those that occur on the heads of children and have earwax, but the section for all these shall be a superficial section.
And pause the puncturing of those in the lower part of them for so that it may be alleviated to the body of the pus in the lower parts.
And then cure those with water and whatever is necessary for its cure.
Forty-second Chapter.
On the Section of Scrofulas which occur in the Neck.
Many times these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, and many that are generated between them, and all scrofula of those is enclosed in its own tunic, and thus as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, just as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for some are lithoid, and some that contain moisture, and some that are malignant, which do not respond to treatment.
Then in that one which you find of good disposition in the Tocament, the color of which is manifest and similar to the cord, and it is spread throughout the whole part, and not annexed to the nerve of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the arteries, and it does not swell, then you should incise that with a superficial section on the upper part of the body from the line .G. to the line .B., and scrape that out of all part, and stretch both lips of the cord once, or several times if you have need of them, just as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And be even more cautious that the vein or nerve is not incised.
And let the spatula not be too sharp, that it does not adjust to your hand and incise, or that it disturbs the patient, and that you do not incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you incise a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from performing the operation, then pause in the wound, and apply vinegar or other powders which stop bleeding, and strengthen the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound becomes softened and begins to heal. When the blood is drained.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire with the patient's said indication if there is any remaining cause of the other little scrofulae, therefore incise those and remove those.
And if it is in the root of a large scrofula, then you should not incise that scrofula from its root. And besides, it is necessary to tie that with a doubled thread tightly and let it remain until it falls off without causing any harm.
And then fill the wound with old cloth which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then cure that.
And if you incise that whole scrofula, then it is necessary to adjust the two lips of the wound and close them as soon as possible so that there is no remaining superfluity.
But if you see that there is remaining superfluity due to the greatness of the scrofula, then it is necessary for you to understand how to incise its root, and extract it and administer suturing and what is said.
And as for the scrofulas that contain moisture, perforate it with a superficial puncture at the location where you see the maturation of the abscess, and pause perforation at the point where the pus is lowest in the body, just as we have said.
And then administer puncture, with linen and resembling those, in order to corrode what remains of the corruption. And then when you see the wound already cleansed, then you should cure it with ointment of flesh-generating substance until it is healed.
Forty-third Chapter.
On the Section of the Pillage of the Abscess which occurs in the Gullet.
The ancients have described this section as the epilhot, and there is not one man in our land who has performed it.
And this is the inscription of the sermon of those.
But in men who have epilepsy, it is necessary to avoid the extraction of the epilhot, because by this means all the veins and main vessels of the patient are here.
But in those who have abscesses in the mouth or gullet or both ears, when the disease is not in the canal, it is necessary to use the section of epilhot to avoid the destruction which is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we should incise the epilhot behind three circles of the epilhot, or four with small section by latitude in that which is between two circles for quantity, so that the section is on the ciphac, not on the cartilage, and this place is suitable for section, because it is deprived of flesh and the vessels of blood are nearby.
And if the doctor is hesitant, then it is necessary to stretch the cord of the gullet once, and then divide the cord into two parts until it reaches the canal, but avoid the blood vessels if you see any cause.
And then incise the ciphac according to what we have recounted; and it is indicated on the section of the canal by the flame which comes from that place with water which is dissipated and by the abscess of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then, when the hour is restored in which puncture is feared, add two lips of the cord to the wound, and close it alone except for the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that make the flesh heal until it is healed.
And let it be known that Albucasis says that the sum of the sermon which we have recounted is nothing but to see if any of these abscesses have opened up the gullet of the patient, and the patient has come to death, and the breath of life is exhausted, and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient can breathe over the site of the wound by some respiration, and thus be saved from death.
And therefore I command to leave the wound open until the malice of the disease is consumed; and it is the malice of life for three days; and then I command thus: close the wound, and cure that which shall be healed.
And what I have seen by my own eyes is this: a servant took a knife and thrust it into their throat and incised a part of the windpipe, and I was called to the cure of life, and I found the patient in such a state as if they were crying out from death, and uncovered the wound and found the blood which had flowed from the wound small.
Therefore I confessed that it had not incised a vein nor anything else, and the breath was coming from the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And there was no accident to the servant, except that his voice was slightly hoarse, and then in some days he returned to improvement of his physical condition.
And now I say to you that there is no fear in the section of the epilhot.
Forty-fourth Chapter.
On the Section of Abscess which occurs in the Gullet outside, and is called Hernia of the Gullet.
This abscess named Hernia of the Gullet is a great abscess according to the color of the body, and it is more common in women.
And it is according to two manners, for either it is natural or accidental.
And in the natural one, there is no need to cure.
And the accidental one is according to two manners of which one is similar to fatty tumors, and the other manner is similar to abscesses that are caused by clogging of an artery, and in that there is fear. And it is not necessary to make assumptions about it unless it is small. And you should then proceed to investigate that with the said method, and if you find it similar to fatty tumors, and it is not suspended from any of the veins, then you should incise it as one would incise nodes, and extract it with water that contains the peel and bursicole; and otherwise, then thoroughly examine all its being, and finally cure the place where it needs curing until it is healed.
Forty-fifth Chapter.
On the Section of the Species of Nodes.
Of nodes many species and I have already said all their species in the division.
And it is necessary to recount thus the difference between a node and an excrescence, lest there be doubt.
Therefore I say that in an excrescence there is heat and fever and violent pains until the ebullition of the superfluity is calmed down; and it is completed putrefaction which then ceases the fever, pain, and heat.
But in a node there is no heat or fever or pain, and it contains that fibrous layer which is at the root of its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a tumor and it is made thus like a sac, whether greater or lesser, and they are according to two species, either fatty and containing moisture, and the colors of the moisture are many, according to what I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the cure of a node, it is necessary to expose it and inquire about its beginning with a sound like that which is called an "intromes", according to what will come in the chapter which is next.
And this is the manner of inquiry for abscesses and all nodes, which is that you take this sound-making instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve the said dits with it little by little until you know that the sound has penetrated the cord.
And then finally your instrument will stop according to the measure of the greatness of the abscess, and you will withdraw the intromes and consider what is left in its wake.
And if the moisture that flows is forgotten, whatever its color may be, then you will incise it with a superficial section according to what I have said in other abscesses.
And if there is no moisture left in the wake of the intromes, then you will know that it is fatty.
And cut on it with a section having the shape of a cross according to this form: just as you have been taught, and suspend it with one thread, and excise the cord of all parts with ease, and preserve the skin or sac, if possible, over that and extract that pus from the node.
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract that pus, and many times you will find that there is nothing left, and then you should extract it little by little until there is no longer any cause from it.
For if there is no longer any cause from it, whether a little or too much, the node will return to its former state.
And if you see that from it there remains a small part, then complete the wound with some sharp and corrosive powders, strengthen the wound, and pause on that which contains the abscess carefully, and cure that by another cure until it is healed.
And if it is a great node, then you should close the two lips of it and cure that with what enfles.
But if the pulse is pulsating in the opposite direction or not pulsating at all, then you should compress and elevate the place of the zone trissat that is vertigo-like and leave it straight for two days or three until the wound is quiet and the ebullition of blood subsides; and then return to incise what remains of the node.

Forty-seventh Chapter.
On the cure of men's breasts which are similar to women's.
Sometimes some men's breasts are inflamed as they begin to be hairy, and then their breasts become swollen, and similar to women's breasts, and apostematous, distant.
And for the one who has this condition, you should incise those with a lunar section according to this form, from the line of B to the line of G, and then excoriate all the hardening, and then fill the wound with an encarnated medicine, and then adjust the two lips of the wound with sutures, and cure that until it is healed.
But if the breast descends to the parts left and is softened by its magnitude, just like in women, then it should be cut into two halves, each of which will be contained within the other, until the major line contains the minor one according to this form from the line of B to the line of G. And then excise the cord that is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the swelling, and administer what we have said about suturing and powders, which are necessary for the cure until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of that which needs to be cut due to the unease of the patient and the flow of blood, then it is necessary to fill the wound with a submersed clot in corrosive oil and leave that until the corrosion that remains from the grayishness is removed, and then cure that until it is healed.
Forty-eighth Chapter.
On the perforation of abscesses that come from left titillating things.
Those abscesses that come from left titillating things are of the species of scrofulas, hard ones, which contain a bag-like skin.
And of those some contain humors; and those that contain humors must be incised with a lunar section according to this figure: from the line of G to the line of B. And when all the pus that is in the abscess has been evacuated, then you should fill it with a fresh clot and leave it until the next day.
And then the clot will be formed and cured with a suitable ointment, and left to heal until it is well.
And if the abscess is of the species of nodes and scrofulas, then you should cut into it as we have said in the section on scrofulas, similarly.
And if the wound is extended in such a way that it produces a pustule, then administer to it a cautery as we have previously said.
Forty-ninth Chapter.
On the section of abscesses that occur through artery or vein.
When the artery is broken and the cord is consolidated which is above it, then many times an abscess occurs. And the signs by which one knows that inflammation and abscess are caused by the artery or vein: for when the abscess is due to an artery it is long aggregated in the preone of the body; and when you press the abscess with those said digits, you feel a stridor within.
And that which is caused by a vein is rounded to the surface of the body.
And the section on this abscess is an error, and most particularly in those that are left behind, and in the armpits, and in the neck, and in many parts of the body; and it is very great, therefore it should be avoided with iron, and that which is in the extremities or at the head, should be avoided.
But in that which is caused by the inflation of the orifice of the artery, you should cut through the cord with a section in length; and then apply the section with incisions and to the artery, and deliver it from its tunics until it is uncovered.
And then apply the needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and strengthen the artery with a double ligature in two places,  which causes you to see the extraction of two arteries that are in the temples.
And then push the place which is between two ligatures with a spatula until all the blood that is in it is released and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer care in the place where pus is generated until the ligatures fall away; and then cure it with suitable ointments applied to it until it is healed.
And if the abscess is due to an incision of a vein, then you should take with your hand as much of the abscess and cord as possible, and then insert the needle into the place where you have held it with your hand, and make it penetrate; and be in that thread double so that you can extract it from the other side, and then ligate the abscess with a single ligature according to what I have said to you about the ligature of two parts with two extremities of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert the needle into the abscess with the first incision of the needle, and strengthen the threads in four places.
And then cut the abscess in the middle of it until when it is situated as it is in you, and then incise the excess of the cord, and leave what remains tied.
And then pause over the powder which you have submerged in vinegar and oil, and then administer the cure which is made with some and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 50: On the abscess that occurs due to twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too does an abscess occur in the nerves when it is struck or caused by excessive labor, and similarly.
And it is most common in the areas of the joints or ankle, or in any place where the joints are affected.
And it is a serious abscess, and is similar to the color of the other flesh, and is in the joints or larger areas where it is located and when it is stretched with great vehemence, one feels like the patient seems stupid.
And it is not an abscess aggregated in the preone of the body, and therefore it is left with the cord, and it is swollen on all sides and does not go to any parts ahead or behind.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be wrapped with iron, for fear of very little contraction.
But that which is on the head or forehead should have the cord removed with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then take it away with forceps and abscesses.
And if it is large, then you should suspend it with an incision, and excise that, and then stop that; and adjust the wound with stitches, and cure that until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
On the section of abscess which occurs due to a blow that hits the stomach.

And I will come again with a memory of cancer right away.
Chapter 52.
On the cure of an Eminence of the Navel.
An Eminence of the Navel is caused by many reasons; either by fissures in the cicatrix which is on the belly, and you see the flesh or budle swelling up according to how it develops in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery, as has been said before, or by constricted wind in it.
But if it is due to a fissure in the cicatrix and swelling of the flesh, then the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the body; and there is little pain, and it appears from different situations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the budle, then the situation of that with water as we have recounted, with even more violent diversity, and when you press it with the said threads, it becomes hidden, and then it returns; and very soon it appears again, and is magnified greatly by the introduction of bath and violent labor.
But if due to moisture, then it is easily felt and not hidden when you press with your hand, nor does it become constricted or swollen.
And if due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears to be declining towards blackness.
But if it is due to nothing but flesh, then it is a serious abscess, large and hard, and it forms according to a quantity.
But if it is due to wind, then the touch of it is easy.
And the operation in this case is that you consider now whether the eminence of the navel is due to blood from an artery, or vein, or wind; then it is necessary that you be prohibited from curing it, for there is fear and terror as I have taught you in the chapter where I spoke about abscesses caused by arteries and veins.
And if the eminence of the navel is due to the budle or flesh, then you must command the patient to hold his breath and be standing upright; and then signal with a tent in the circuit of all the navel, and then command him to stay still between your hands on both sides of his back, and then circle around with a spatula flat in the circuit of the navel over the place which you have signalled with the tent; and then extend most of the abscess into the parts above with a large thread; and then bind it in the circuit with a strong or soft ligature and the manner of binding decent.
And then apply the middle of the abscess extended over the ligature and insert the said thread into it and seek the budle; and if you have found or guarded it by the ligature, then take out the altarated and push the budle into the parts below the belly.
And if you have found the flesh, then take it with a thread; and cut out the excesses of it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, then circle around that point, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and insert them into those two strong threads and enter the two needles in the circuit which you have made around the abscess in a cross-like manner, so that they will penetrate; and then strengthen the abscess in four parts above the needles.
And if you wish, stop the needles and leave the place until the flesh stretched out and falls away by itself; or cut into it when it is softened and becomes pliable, and then cure that with water as necessary for ointments until it is healed.
And if the eminence of the navel is due to nothing but flesh or moisture in it, then you must conquer the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or moisture which you find there, and then cure that with water as necessary and consolidate the place.

Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Be aware that castration in our law is excommunicated, but for a reason it is written to me that I remember it in this book; and it is not necessary to recall it except by two methods, of which one is because this is in the science of the doctor when he is questioned about it, knowing the cure of him who suffers from it; and the other method is that many times we have needed castration of other animals for our own use through those animals, just as with horses, bulls, pigs, and similar beasts.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by castration, or by incision with a section.
But in that which is done by castration, the method of the operation is that we make the animal lie down in cold water until the testicles of it are softened and relaxed and hang. And then take those with your right hand into which they are resolved and do not appear to be left behind the touchment.
But in castration done by incision and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the cord of the testicles with your left hand.
And then tie the suspenders, and cut on each humor with one section until two humors process.
And then cut those more than you would have excoriated. And do not leave any cause on those of the cicatrix, but the subtle cicatrix which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better which is done by castration, for castration leaves no cause of the testicles so that the animal desires to copulate.
And then cure the wound until it is healed.
